  <div class="pane-content">
    <div class="highwire-markup"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="content-block-markup" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"><div class="article fulltext-view "><span class="highwire-journal-article-marker-start"></span><div class="contributors"><ol class="contributor-list" id="contrib-group-1"><li class="contributor" id="contrib-1"><span class="name">Ju-Sheng Zheng</span><span class="contrib-role">, PhD student</span><a id="xref-aff-1-1" class="xref-aff bmjAuthorAffiliatesMarkupProcessor-processed" href="#aff-1">1</a><a id="xref-aff-2-1" class="xref-aff bmjAuthorAffiliatesMarkupProcessor-processed" href="#aff-2">2</a>, </li><li class="contributor" id="contrib-2"><span class="name">Xiao-Jie Hu</span><span class="contrib-role">, PhD student</span><a id="xref-aff-1-2" class="xref-aff bmjAuthorAffiliatesMarkupProcessor-processed" href="#aff-1">1</a><a id="xref-aff-2-2" class="xref-aff bmjAuthorAffiliatesMarkupProcessor-processed" href="#aff-2">2</a>, </li><li class="contributor" id="contrib-3"><span class="name">Yi-Min Zhao</span><span class="contrib-role">, masters student</span><a id="xref-aff-1-3" class="xref-aff bmjAuthorAffiliatesMarkupProcessor-processed" href="#aff-1">1</a><a id="xref-aff-2-3" class="xref-aff bmjAuthorAffiliatesMarkupProcessor-processed" href="#aff-2">2</a>, </li><li class="contributor" id="contrib-4"><span class="name">Jing Yang</span><span class="contrib-role">, PhD student</span><a id="xref-aff-1-4" class="xref-aff bmjAuthorAffiliatesMarkupProcessor-processed" href="#aff-1">1</a><a id="xref-aff-2-4" class="xref-aff bmjAuthorAffiliatesMarkupProcessor-processed" href="#aff-2">2</a>, </li><li class="last" id="contrib-5"><span class="name">Duo Li</span><span class="contrib-role">, professor</span><a id="xref-aff-1-5" class="xref-aff bmjAuthorAffiliatesMarkupProcessor-processed" href="#aff-1">1</a><a id="xref-aff-2-5" class="xref-aff bmjAuthorAffiliatesMarkupProcessor-processed" href="#aff-2">2</a></li></ol><ol class="affiliation-list-reveal"><a href="#" class="view-more">Author affiliations</a></ol><ol class="affiliation-list bmjAuthorAffiliatesMarkupProcessor-processed" style="display: none;"><li class="aff"><a id="aff-1" name="aff-1"></a><address><sup>1</sup>Department of Food Science and Nutrition, Zhejiang University, Hangzhou 310058, China</address></li><li class="aff"><a id="aff-2" name="aff-2"></a><address><sup>2</sup>APCNS Center of Nutrition and Food Safety, Hangzhou 310058, China</address></li></ol><ol class="corresp-list"><li class="corresp" id="corresp-1">Correspondence to: D Li, Department of Food Science and Nutrition, Zhejiang University, 866 Yuhangtang Road, Hangzhou 310058, China <span class="em-link"><span class="em-addr"><a href="mailto:duoli@zju.edu.cn">duoli@zju.edu.cn</a></span></span></li></ol><ul class="history-list"><li xmlns:hwp="http://schema.highwire.org/Journal" class="accepted" hwp:start="2013-05-31"><span class="accepted-label">Accepted </span>31 May 2013</li></ul></div><div class="section abstract" id="abstract-1"><h2>Abstract</h2><p id="p-2"><strong>Objectives</strong> To investigate the association between intake of fish and n-3 polyunsaturated fatty acids (n-3 PUFA) and the risk of breast cancer and to evaluate the potential dose-response relation.</p><p id="p-3"><strong>Design</strong> Meta-analysis and systematic review of prospective cohort studies.</p><p id="p-4"><strong>Data sources</strong> PubMed and Embase up to December 2012 and references of retrieved relevant articles.</p><p id="p-5"><strong>Eligibility criteria for selecting studies</strong> Prospective cohort studies with relative risk and 95% confidence intervals for breast cancer according to fish intake, n-3 PUFA intake, or tissue biomarkers.</p><p id="p-6"><strong>Results</strong> Twenty six publications, including 20 905 cases of breast cancer and 883 585 participants from 21 independent prospective cohort studies were eligible. Eleven articles (13 323 breast cancer events and 687 770 participants) investigated fish intake, 17 articles investigated marine n-3 PUFA (16 178 breast cancer events and 527 392 participants), and 12 articles investigated alpha linolenic acid (14 284 breast cancer events and 405 592 participants). Marine n-3 PUFA was associated with 14% reduction of risk of breast cancer (relative risk for highest <em>v</em> lowest category 0.86 (95% confidence interval 0.78 to 0.94), I<sup>2</sup>=54), and the relative risk remained similar whether marine n-3 PUFA was measured as dietary intake (0.85, 0.76 to 0.96, I<sup>2</sup>=67%) or as tissue biomarkers (0.86, 0.71 to 1.03, I<sup>2</sup>=8%). Subgroup analyses also indicated that the inverse association between marine n-3 PUFA and risk was more evident in studies that did not adjust for body mass index (BMI) (0.74, 0.64 to 0.86, I<sup>2</sup>=0) than in studies that did adjust for BMI (0.90, 0.80 to 1.01, I<sup>2</sup>=63.2%). Dose-response analysis indicated that risk of breast cancer was reduced by 5% per 0.1g/day (0.95, 0.90 to 1.00, I<sup>2</sup>=52%) or 0.1% energy/day (0.95, 0.90 to 1.00, I<sup>2</sup>=79%) increment of dietary marine n-3 PUFA intake. No significant association was observed for fish intake or exposure to alpha linolenic acid.</p><p id="p-7"><strong>Conclusions</strong> Higher consumption of dietary marine n-3 PUFA is associated with a lower risk of breast cancer. The associations of fish and alpha linolenic acid intake with risk warrant further investigation of prospective cohort studies. These findings could have public health implications with regard to prevention of breast cancer through dietary and lifestyle interventions.</p></div><div class="section intro" id="sec-1"><h2>Introduction</h2><p id="p-8">Breast cancer is one of the most common cancers and the leading cause of death from cancer among women, accounting for 23% of the total cancer cases and 14% of cancer deaths in 2008.<a id="xref-ref-1-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-1" rel="#ref-1~.ref-cit">1</a> For the past few decades, epidemiological studies<a id="xref-ref-2-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-2" rel="#ref-2~.ref-cit">2</a> <a id="xref-ref-3-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-3" rel="#ref-3~.ref-cit">3</a> have suggested that a healthy diet and lifestyle is critical for the prevention of breast cancer, and dietary fat is one of the most intensively studied dietary factors closely related with risk.<a id="xref-ref-4-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-4" rel="#ref-4~.ref-cit">4</a> <a id="xref-ref-5-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-5" rel="#ref-5~.ref-cit">5</a> <a id="xref-ref-6-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-6" rel="#ref-6~.ref-cit">6</a> <a id="xref-ref-7-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-7" rel="#ref-7~.ref-cit">7</a> <a id="xref-ref-8-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-8" rel="#ref-8~.ref-cit">8</a> Among subtypes of dietary fat, n-3 polyunsaturated fatty acids (n-3 PUFA) are the most promising types to inhibit or curtail carcinogenesis and reduce risk, as shown in rodent models<a id="xref-ref-9-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-9" rel="#ref-9~.ref-cit">9</a> <a id="xref-ref-10-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-10" rel="#ref-10~.ref-cit">10</a> and with in vitro cell studies.<a id="xref-ref-11-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-11" rel="#ref-11~.ref-cit">11</a> Results from observational studies in humans, however, are inconsistent. Several large prospective cohort studies, such as the Singapore Chinese Health Study<a id="xref-ref-12-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-12" rel="#ref-12~.ref-cit">12</a> and the Japan Collaborative Cohort Study,<a id="xref-ref-5-2" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-5" rel="#ref-5~.ref-cit">5</a> have suggested an inverse association between dietary n-3 PUFA intake and risk. Furthermore, several case-control studies have indicated that n-3 PUFA, measured as either dietary intake or with tissue biomarkers, is inversely associated with risk.<a id="xref-ref-13-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-13" rel="#ref-13~.ref-cit">13</a> <a id="xref-ref-14-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-14" rel="#ref-14~.ref-cit">14</a> <a id="xref-ref-15-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-15" rel="#ref-15~.ref-cit">15</a> <a id="xref-ref-16-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-16" rel="#ref-16~.ref-cit">16</a> Most of the other cohort or case-control studies, however, found no association between n-3 PUFA and risk.<a id="xref-ref-8-2" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-8" rel="#ref-8~.ref-cit">8</a> <a id="xref-ref-17-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-17" rel="#ref-17~.ref-cit">17</a> <a id="xref-ref-18-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-18" rel="#ref-18~.ref-cit">18</a> <a id="xref-ref-19-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-19" rel="#ref-19~.ref-cit">19</a> <a id="xref-ref-20-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-20" rel="#ref-20~.ref-cit">20</a> <a id="xref-ref-21-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-21" rel="#ref-21~.ref-cit">21</a> <a id="xref-ref-22-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-22" rel="#ref-22~.ref-cit">22</a> <a id="xref-ref-23-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-23" rel="#ref-23~.ref-cit">23</a> <a id="xref-ref-24-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-24" rel="#ref-24~.ref-cit">24</a> <a id="xref-ref-25-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-25" rel="#ref-25~.ref-cit">25</a> <a id="xref-ref-26-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-26" rel="#ref-26~.ref-cit">26</a> <a id="xref-ref-27-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-27" rel="#ref-27~.ref-cit">27</a> Moreover, accumulating prospective studies suggest that fish, the richest source of marine n-3 PUFA, shows inverse,<a id="xref-ref-12-2" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-12" rel="#ref-12~.ref-cit">12</a> <a id="xref-ref-28-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-28" rel="#ref-28~.ref-cit">28</a> <a id="xref-ref-29-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-29" rel="#ref-29~.ref-cit">29</a> null,<a id="xref-ref-5-3" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-5" rel="#ref-5~.ref-cit">5</a> <a id="xref-ref-20-2" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-20" rel="#ref-20~.ref-cit">20</a> <a id="xref-ref-21-2" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-21" rel="#ref-21~.ref-cit">21</a> <a id="xref-ref-30-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-30" rel="#ref-30~.ref-cit">30</a> <a id="xref-ref-31-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-31" rel="#ref-31~.ref-cit">31</a> or even positive<a id="xref-ref-32-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-32" rel="#ref-32~.ref-cit">32</a> <a id="xref-ref-33-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-33" rel="#ref-33~.ref-cit">33</a> associations with risk. It is therefore important and interesting to quantitatively assess the association between intake of n-3 PUFA and fish and the risk of breast cancer from the available evidence.</p><p id="p-9">We used meta-analysis to summarise the associations between dietary intake of fish and n-3 PUFA with incident breast cancer based on prospective cohort studies. We pooled risk estimates for the highest versus lowest category of intake (or tissue biomarkers) across identified prospective cohort studies to examine the overall association. We conducted a dose-response analysis for the trend estimation and a stratified analysis to examine the sources of heterogeneity.</p></div><div class="section methods" id="sec-2"><h2>Methods</h2><div id="sec-3" class="subsection"><h3>Search strategy and selection criteria</h3><p id="p-10">We followed the criteria for conducting and reporting meta-analysis of observational studies.<a id="xref-ref-34-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-34" rel="#ref-34~.ref-cit">34</a> A systematic search was conducted in two databases—PubMed and Embase—up to December 2012. We used the following key words treated as title/abstract for the literature search: (“fat” OR “fatty acid” OR “docosahexaenoic acid” OR “eicosapentaenoic acid” OR “docosapentaenoic acid” OR “alpha-linolenic acid” OR “polyunsaturated fatty acid” OR “omega-3 fatty acid” OR “n-3 fatty acid” OR “fish” OR “fish oil” OR “seafood”) AND (“breast cancer” OR “breast neoplasms”). Full details of the search strategy are in appendix 1. Our search was restricted to studies in humans and studies published in English. The references of retrieved relevant articles were reviewed to identify potential publications. We did not contact authors for the detailed information of primary studies.</p><p id="p-11">Two investigators (J-SZ and X-JH) independently conducted the literature search, identified potential studies, and extracted detailed information from each included article. Discrepancies were resolved through group discussion with the third investigator (DL). Inclusion criteria were prospective study design (including prospective cohort, nested case-control, and case-cohort studies); the exposure of interest was any type of dietary n-3 PUFA or fish consumption or tissue n-3 PUFA concentrations; the endpoint of interest was incident breast cancer in women; and the risk estimate with corresponding 95% confidence intervals of breast cancer was reported for n-3 PUFA exposure or fish intake. We excluded retrospective or cross sectional studies, studies in animals, non-original research (reviews, editorials, or commentaries), abstracts, unpublished studies, and duplicated studies.</p></div><div id="sec-4" class="subsection"><h3>Data extraction</h3><p id="p-12">From each identified article, we extracted the first author’s name, study population and region, study design, duration of follow-up, age of participants, number of cases and non-cases, person years for the population and for each exposure category, risk estimates and corresponding 95% confidence intervals for each category of n-3 PUFA or fish intake, menopausal status, method of n-3 PUFA measurement (diet or tissue biomarker), and covariates. We extracted risk estimates with the most adjustment.</p><p id="p-13">Quality assessment was conducted according to the Newcastle-Ottawa criteria<a id="xref-ref-35-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-35" rel="#ref-35~.ref-cit">35</a> for non-randomised studies. A maximum of 9 points was assigned to each study: 4 for selection, 2 for comparability, and 3 for assessment of outcomes (for cohort study) or exposures (for case-control study). We regarded scores of 0-3, 4-6, and 7-9 as low, moderate, and high quality, respectively.</p></div><div id="sec-5" class="subsection"><h3>Data synthesis</h3><p id="p-14">We used relative risk for risk estimates, and hazard ratios in cohort studies and odds ratios in nested case-control studies were treated as relative risks directly. We used log transformed relative risk and its corresponding 95% confidence interval from each eligible study for the meta-analysis. As different studies might use different assessment methods (diet or tissue biomarkers) and report different exposure categories (dichotomous, thirds, quarters, or fifths), we used the study specific relative risk for the highest versus lowest category of fish consumption or n-3 PUFA exposure for the meta-analysis. We then combined the relative risk from each study, weighted by the inverse of their variance, for the meta-analysis with the DerSimonian and Laird random effects model, which takes variation both within and between studies into consideration.<a id="xref-ref-36-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-36" rel="#ref-36~.ref-cit">36</a> Studies that reported relative risk of breast cancer separately for postmenopausal and premenopausal women<a id="xref-ref-12-3" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-12" rel="#ref-12~.ref-cit">12</a> <a id="xref-ref-19-2" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-19" rel="#ref-19~.ref-cit">19</a> <a id="xref-ref-30-2" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-30" rel="#ref-30~.ref-cit">30</a> <a id="xref-ref-31-2" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-31" rel="#ref-31~.ref-cit">31</a> were considered as independent studies for the meta-analysis. Women in one study were reported as either premenopausal or perimenopausal,<a id="xref-ref-17-2" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-17" rel="#ref-17~.ref-cit">17</a> and thus we classed them all as premenopausal. One study in which only 10% of women with breast cancer were premenopausal<a id="xref-ref-20-3" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-20" rel="#ref-20~.ref-cit">20</a> was treated as a postmenopausal study. Most of the women with breast cancer in another study were postmenopausal,<a id="xref-ref-23-2" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-23" rel="#ref-23~.ref-cit">23</a> and we treated this study as a postmenopausal study.</p><p id="p-15">We conducted meta-analysis for different types of n-3 PUFA separately. Firstly, we estimated the pooled relative risk between the highest versus lowest category of fish intake, total marine n-3 PUFA, alpha linolenic acid (ALA), and total n-3 PUFA, respectively. For studies that did not report a relative risk for total marine n-3 PUFA but that reported risks for eicosapentaenoic acid (EPA), docosahexaenoic acid (DHA), and docosapentaenoic acid (DPA) separately,<a id="xref-ref-8-3" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-8" rel="#ref-8~.ref-cit">8</a> <a id="xref-ref-17-3" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-17" rel="#ref-17~.ref-cit">17</a> <a id="xref-ref-18-2" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-18" rel="#ref-18~.ref-cit">18</a> <a id="xref-ref-19-3" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-19" rel="#ref-19~.ref-cit">19</a> <a id="xref-ref-24-2" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-24" rel="#ref-24~.ref-cit">24</a> <a id="xref-ref-37-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-37" rel="#ref-37~.ref-cit">37</a> <a id="xref-ref-38-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-38" rel="#ref-38~.ref-cit">38</a> we pooled these relative risks to represent the relative risk of total marine n-3 PUFA exposure. One study reported relative risks for intake of dried and non-dried fish separately,<a id="xref-ref-29-2" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-29" rel="#ref-29~.ref-cit">29</a> and we pooled the two relative risks with a fixed effects model to get a summary relative risk for total fish intake in this study for further meta-analysis. Secondly, for marine n-3 PUFA, we conducted meta-analysis for EPA, DHA, and EPA separately.</p><p id="p-16">We carried out a dose-response analysis for the trend estimation using generalised least squares regression (two stage GLST in Stata).<a id="xref-ref-39-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-39" rel="#ref-39~.ref-cit">39</a> For a study without information on the number of cases, number of healthy controls, or person years for exposure categories, we used variance weighted least squares regression for the dose-response estimation. Studies with fewer than three exposure categories were excluded from trend estimation. Dose-response analysis was conducted only in studies that reported dietary intake of n-3 PUFA or fish intake because the results of tissue n-3 PUFA compositions varied according to different tissues (serum, erythrocytes, or adipose) and units used and were not appropriate for standardisation. For fish intake, all the different units were transformed to g/day as described.<a id="xref-ref-40-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-40" rel="#ref-40~.ref-cit">40</a> One study<a id="xref-ref-12-4" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-12" rel="#ref-12~.ref-cit">12</a> reported the unit for the fish intake category as g/1000 kcal, which we transformed to g/day assuming an average energy intake of 2000 kcal/day in this population. For dietary n-3 PUFA, we carried out dose-response analysis among studies with exposure units as % energy/day and g/day, separately. We did not do trend estimation for EPA, DHA, DPA, or total n-3 PUFA because there were limited studies with dietary information for these exposures. To estimate a potential curve linear association between fish, dietary n-3 PUFA, and breast cancer, we used a restricted cubic spline model (three knots).</p><p id="p-17">Study heterogeneity was estimated with I<sup>2</sup> statistic, with values of 25%, 50%, and 75% representing low, moderate, and high degrees of heterogeneity. Subgroup analysis was conducted to examine sources of study heterogeneity and the influence of potential residual confounding factors, such as age, body mass index (BMI), total energy intake, and education. Univariate meta-regression was performed to examine the significance of the difference in relative risks by different subgroups, including study region (Asian countries <em>v</em> western countries), duration of follow-up (lower <em>v</em> higher than mean duration), exposure measurement (diet <em>v</em> tissue biomarker), study type (cohort <em>v</em> nested case-control), menopausal status (premenopausal <em>v</em> postmenopausal), study quality (score ≤7 <em>v</em> &gt;7), risk expression (hazard/rate ratio, relative risk, or odds ratio), and adjustment of covariates (including age, BMI, total energy intake, and education). Sensitivity analysis was conducted by omitting one study at a time and examining the influence of each individual study on the overall relative risk. Publication bias was evaluated by visual inspection of a funnel plot and Egger’s regression test (significant at P&lt;0.1). We used a trim and fill algorithm<a id="xref-ref-41-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-41" rel="#ref-41~.ref-cit">41</a> if possible publication bias was detected to identify and correct for the asymmetry of funnel plot from publication bias and provide an adjusted summary relative risk based on all the studies, including the estimated missing studies. Stata version 12 (StataCorp LP, College Station, TX, USA) was used for all the statistical analyses.</p></div></div><div class="section results" id="sec-6"><h2>Results</h2><div id="sec-7" class="subsection"><h3>Literature search</h3><p id="p-18">Through full text examination of 215 potential publications we identified 26 eligible publications, including 20 905 cases of breast cancer and 883 585 participants, from 21 independent prospective cohort studies (fig 1<a id="xref-fig-1-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#F1"><span>⇓</span></a>). Among these articles, 11 were from the United States,<a id="xref-ref-6-2" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-6" rel="#ref-6~.ref-cit">6</a> <a id="xref-ref-8-4" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-8" rel="#ref-8~.ref-cit">8</a> <a id="xref-ref-19-4" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-19" rel="#ref-19~.ref-cit">19</a> <a id="xref-ref-20-4" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-20" rel="#ref-20~.ref-cit">20</a> <a id="xref-ref-21-3" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-21" rel="#ref-21~.ref-cit">21</a> <a id="xref-ref-24-3" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-24" rel="#ref-24~.ref-cit">24</a> <a id="xref-ref-30-3" class="xref-bibr rel="#ref-42~.ref-cit">42</a> <a id="xref-ref-43-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-43" rel="#ref-43~.ref-cit">43</a> 11 from Europe,<a id="xref-ref-17-4" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-17" rel="#ref-17~.ref-cit">17</a> <a id="xref-ref-18-3" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-18" rel="#ref-18~.ref-cit">18</a> <a id="xref-ref-23-3" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-23" rel="#ref-23~.ref-cit">23</a> <a id="xref-ref-25-2" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-25" rel="#ref-37~.ref-cit">37</a> <a id="xref-ref-44-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-44" rel="#ref-44~.ref-cit">44</a> <a id="xref-ref-45-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-45" rel="#ref-45~.ref-cit">45</a> and four from Asia.<a id="xref-ref-5-4" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-5" rel="#ref-5~.ref-cit">5</a> <a id="xref-ref-12-5" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-12" rel="#ref-12~.ref-cit">12</a> <a id="xref-ref-27-2" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-27" rel="#ref-27~.ref-cit">27</a> <a id="xref-ref-29-3" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-29" rel="#ref-29~.ref-cit">29</a> Eleven articles described the association between fish intake and risk of breast cancer,<a id="xref-ref-5-5" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-5" rel="#ref-5~.ref-cit">5</a> <a id="xref-ref-12-6" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-12" rel="#ref-12~.ref-cit">12</a> <a id="xref-ref-20-5" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-20" rel="#ref-20~.ref-cit">20</a> <a id="xref-ref-21-4" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-21" rel="#ref-21~.ref-cit">21</a> <a id="xref-ref-28-3" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-28" rel="#ref-28~.ref-cit">28</a> <a id="xref-ref-29-4" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-29" rel="#ref-29~.ref-cit">29</a> <a id="xref-ref-30-4" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-30" rel="#ref-30~.ref-cit">30</a> <a id="xref-ref-31-4" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-31" rel="#ref-31~.ref-cit">31</a> <a id="xref-ref-32-3" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-32" rel="#ref-32~.ref-cit">32</a> <a id="xref-ref-33-3" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-33" rel="#ref-33~.ref-cit">33</a> <a id="xref-ref-42-2" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-42" rel="#ref-42~.ref-cit">42</a> 17 described the association between marine n-3 PUFA intake and risk,<a id="xref-ref-5-6" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-5" rel="#ref-5~.ref-cit">5</a> <a id="xref-ref-8-5" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-8" rel="#ref-8~.ref-cit">8</a> <a id="xref-ref-12-7" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-12" rel="#ref-12~.ref-cit">12</a> <a id="xref-ref-17-5" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-17" rel="#ref-17~.ref-cit">17</a> <a id="xref-ref-18-4" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-18" rel="#ref-18~.ref-cit">18</a> <a id="xref-ref-19-5" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-19" rel="#ref-19~.ref-cit">19</a> <a id="xref-ref-20-6" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-20" rel="#ref-20~.ref-cit">20</a> <a id="xref-ref-21-5" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-21" rel="#ref-21~.ref-cit">21</a> <a id="xref-ref-23-4" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-23" rel="#ref-23~.ref-cit">23</a> <a id="xref-ref-24-4" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-24" rel="#ref-24~.ref-cit">24</a> <a id="xref-ref-25-3" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-25" rel="#ref-25~.ref-cit">25</a> <a id="xref-ref-26-3" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-26" rel="#ref-26~.ref-cit">26</a> <a id="xref-ref-27-3" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-27" rel="#ref-27~.ref-cit">27</a> <a id="xref-ref-37-3" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-37" rel="#ref-37~.ref-cit">37</a> <a id="xref-ref-38-3" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-38" rel="#ref-38~.ref-cit">38</a> <a id="xref-ref-43-2" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-43" rel="#ref-43~.ref-cit">43</a> <a id="xref-ref-44-2" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-44" rel="#ref-44~.ref-cit">44</a> 12 reported the association between ALA and risk,<a id="xref-ref-8-6" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-8" rel="#ref-8~.ref-cit">8</a> <a id="xref-ref-12-8" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-12" rel="#ref-12~.ref-cit">12</a> <a id="xref-ref-17-6" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-17" rel="#ref-17~.ref-cit">17</a> <a id="xref-ref-18-5" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-18" rel="#ref-18~.ref-cit">18</a> <a id="xref-ref-19-6" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-19" rel="#ref-19~.ref-cit">19</a> <a id="xref-ref-23-5" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-23" rel="#ref-23~.ref-cit">23</a> <a id="xref-ref-24-5" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-24" rel="#ref-24~.ref-cit">24</a> <a id="xref-ref-25-4" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-25" rel="#ref-25~.ref-cit">25</a> <a id="xref-ref-27-4" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-27" rel="#ref-27~.ref-cit">27</a> <a id="xref-ref-37-4" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-37" rel="#ref-37~.ref-cit">37</a> <a id="xref-ref-38-4" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-38" rel="#ref-38~.ref-cit">38</a> <a id="xref-ref-44-3" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-44" rel="#ref-44~.ref-cit">44</a> and 10 described the association between total n-3 PUFA and risk.<a id="xref-ref-5-7" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-5" rel="#ref-5~.ref-cit">5</a> <a id="xref-ref-8-7" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-8" rel="#ref-8~.ref-cit">8</a> <a id="xref-ref-12-9" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-12" rel="#ref-12~.ref-cit">12</a> <a id="xref-ref-18-6" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-18" rel="#ref-18~.ref-cit">18</a> <a id="xref-ref-19-7" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-19" rel="#ref-19~.ref-cit">19</a> <a id="xref-ref-23-6" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-23" rel="#ref-23~.ref-cit">23</a> <a id="xref-ref-24-6" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-24" rel="#ref-24~.ref-cit">24</a> <a id="xref-ref-25-5" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-25" rel="#ref-25~.ref-cit">25</a> <a id="xref-ref-44-4" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-44" rel="#ref-44~.ref-cit">44</a> <a id="xref-ref-45-2" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-45" rel="#ref-45~.ref-cit">45</a></p><div id="F1" class="fig pos-float  odd"><div class="fig-inline"><div class="highwire-figure colorbox"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="http://static.www.bmj.com/content/bmj/346/bmj.f3706/F1.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600" title="Fig 1 Flow diagram for selection of studies in meta-analysis of intake of polyunsaturated fatty <li class="2 last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/185148" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt">Download powerpoint</a></li>
</ul></div></div><div class="fig-caption" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"></div></div></div><div id="sec-8" class="subsection"><h3>Study characteristics</h3><p id="p-20">The 26 eligible articles were prospective cohort studies with mean follow-up ranging from 4.3 to 20 years. Among these, eight were nested case-control studies<a id="xref-ref-17-7" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-17" rel="#ref-17~.ref-cit">17</a> <a id="xref-ref-18-7" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-18" rel="#ref-18~.ref-cit">18</a> <a id="xref-ref-19-8" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-19" rel="#ref-19~.ref-cit">19</a> <a id="xref-ref-23-7" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-23" rel="#ref-23~.ref-cit">23</a> <a id="xref-ref-24-7" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-24" rel="#ref-24~.ref-cit">24</a> <a id="xref-ref-42-3" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-42" rel="#ref-42~.ref-cit">42</a> <a id="xref-ref-44-5" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-44" rel="#ref-44~.ref-cit">44</a> <a id="xref-ref-45-3" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-45" rel="#ref-45~.ref-cit">45</a> and two were case-cohort studies<a id="xref-ref-26-4" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-26" rel="#ref-26~.ref-cit">26</a> <a id="xref-ref-37-5" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-37" rel="#ref-37~.ref-cit">37</a> (see table A in appendix 2 and table B in appendix 3). For two cohorts, relative risks for fish intake and n-3 PUFA were reported in two different publications.<a id="xref-ref-19-9" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-19" rel="#ref-19~.ref-cit">19</a> <a id="xref-ref-26-5" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-26" rel="#ref-26~.ref-cit">26</a> <a id="xref-ref-33-4" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-33" rel="#ref-33~.ref-cit">33</a> <a id="xref-ref-42-4" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-42" rel="#ref-42~.ref-cit">42</a> For one cohort, the relative risks for fish intake, n-3 PUFA intake, and tissue n-3 PUFA concentrations were reported in three different articles.<a id="xref-ref-23-8" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-23" rel="#ref-23~.ref-cit">23</a> <a id="xref-ref-25-6" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-25" rel="#ref-25~.ref-cit">25</a> <a id="xref-ref-31-5" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-31" rel="#ref-31~.ref-cit">31</a> One study reported the relative risk for additional breast cancer events in a cohort of women who had received a diagnosis and been treated for early stage breast cancer.<a id="xref-ref-43-3" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-43" rel="#ref-43~.ref-cit">43</a> One study reported only the relative risk for the percentage change of dietary n-3 PUFA,<a id="xref-ref-6-3" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-6" rel="#ref-6~.ref-cit">6</a> and thus it was included only in the dose-response analysis. The average score for the quality assessment of included studies was 7.6, and the score for all the studies was 5 or above (moderate or high quality) (see supplemental table C in appendix 3).</p></div><div id="sec-9" class="subsection"><h3>Fish consumption and risk of breast cancer </h3><p id="p-21">Eleven studies from 11 independent cohorts reported an association between fish intake and risk of breast cancer, with 13 323 breast cancer events and 687 770 participants. Overall, fish intake was not associated with risk (relative risk 1.03, 95% confidence interval 0.93 to 1.14) (fig 2<a id="xref-fig-2-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#F2"><span>⇓</span></a>). There was moderate study heterogeneity (I<sup>2</sup>=54%). No publication bias was observed from the funnel plot (see supplemental fig A in appendix 4) or Eggers test (P=0.6).</p><div id="F2" class="fig pos-float  odd"><div class="fig-inline"><div class="highwire-figure colorbox"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="http://static.www.bmj.com/content/bmj/346/bmj.f3706/F2.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600" title="Fig 2 Relative risk of breast cancer for highest v lowest category of dietary fish intake. Overall relative risk calculated with random effects model" class="colorbox fragment-images colorbox-load init-colorbox-processed cboxElement" rel="gallery-fragment-images" data-figure-caption="&amp;lt;div class=&amp;quot;fig-caption&amp;quot; xmlns:xhtml=&amp;quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&amp;quot;&amp;gt;&amp;lt;/div&amp;gt;"><img class="fragment-image" src="http://static.www.bmj.com/content/bmj/346/bmj.f3706/F2.medium.gif" alt="Figure2" data-original-title="" title="" /></a></div></div><div xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" class="fig-caption"><p id="p-22" class="first-child"><strong>Fig 2</strong> Relative risk of breast cancer for highest <em>v</em> lowest category of dietary fish intake. Overall relative risk calculated with random effects model</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links list-inline"><li class="0 first"><a href="http://static.www.bmj.com/content/bmj/346/bmj.f3706/F2.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Figure2">Download figure</a></li>
<li class="1"><a href="http://static.www.bmj.com/content/bmj/346/bmj.f3706/F2.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank">Open in new tab</a></li>
<li class="2 last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/185149" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt">Download powerpoint</a></li>
</ul></div></div><div class="fig-caption" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"></div></div><p id="p-23">All eleven studies were eligible for the trend estimation. Dose-response analysis found no association with risk of breast cancer per 15 g/day increment of fish intake (relative risk 1.00, 95% confidence interval 0.97 to 1.03) (see supplemental fig B in appendix 4). No publication bias was observed. We did not find a curvilinear association between fish intake and risk (P=0.22 for non-linearity) (see supplemental fig C in appendix 4).</p></div><div id="sec-10" class="subsection"><h3>Marine n-3 PUFA and risk of breast cancer </h3><p id="p-24">Seventeen articles from 16 independent cohort studies reported the association between marine n-3 PUFA and risk of breast cancer, involving 16 178 breast cancer events and 527 392 participants. Marine n-3 PUFA was inversely associated with risk (relative risk 0.86, 95% confidence interval 0.78 to 0.94; I<sup>2</sup>=54%) (fig 3<a id="xref-fig-3-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#F3"><span>⇓</span></a>). The funnel plot (see supplemental fig D in appendix 4) and Eggers test (P=0.017) indicated slight publication bias. Trim and fill analysis, however, did not change the result.</p><div id="F3" class="fig pos-float  odd"><div class="fig-inline"><div class="highwire-figure colorbox"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="http://static.www.bmj.com/content/bmj/346/bmj.f3706/F3.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600" title="Fig 3 Relative risk of breast cancer for highest v lowest category of marine n-3 PUFA. Overall relative risk calculated with random effects model" class="colorbox fragment-images colorbox-load init-colorbox-processed cboxElement" rel="gallery-fragment-images" data-figure-caption="&amp;lt;div class=&amp;quot;fig-caption&amp;quot; xmlns:xhtml=&amp;quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&amp;quot;&amp;gt;&amp;lt;/div&amp;gt;"><img class="fragment-image" src="http://static.www.bmj.com/content/bmj/346/bmj.f3706/F3.medium.gif" alt="Figure3" data-original-title="" title="" /></a></div></div><div xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" class="fig-caption"><p id="p-25" class="first-child"><strong>Fig 3</strong> Relative risk of breast cancer for highest <em>v</em> lowest category of marine n-3 PUFA. Overall relative risk calculated with random effects model</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links list-inline"><li class="0 first"><a href="http://static.www.bmj.com/content/bmj/346/bmj.f3706/F3.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Figure3">Download figure</a></li>
<li class="1"><a href="http://static.www.bmj.com/content/bmj/346/bmj.f3706/F3.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank">Open in new tab</a></li>

</ul></div></div><div class="fig-caption" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"></div></div><p id="p-26">Eight articles were eligible for the dose-response analysis of dietary marine n-3 PUFA and risk of breast cancer.<a id="xref-ref-5-8" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-5" rel="#ref-5~.ref-cit">5</a> <a id="xref-ref-6-4" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-6" rel="#ref-6~.ref-cit">6</a> <a id="xref-ref-12-10" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-12" rel="#ref-12~.ref-cit">12</a> <a id="xref-ref-20-7" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-20" rel="#ref-20~.ref-cit">20</a> <a id="xref-ref-21-6" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-21" rel="#ref-21~.ref-cit">21</a> <a id="xref-ref-25-7" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-25" rel="#ref-25~.ref-cit">25</a> <a id="xref-ref-27-5" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-27" rel="#ref-27~.ref-cit">27</a> <a id="xref-ref-43-4" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-43" rel="#ref-43~.ref-cit">43</a> Three studies reported marine n-3 PUFA as g/day,<a id="xref-ref-21-7" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-21" rel="#ref-21~.ref-cit">21</a> <a id="xref-ref-27-6" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-27" rel="#ref-27~.ref-cit">27</a> <a id="xref-ref-43-5" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-43" rel="#ref-43~.ref-cit">43</a> while the five other studies<a id="xref-ref-5-9" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-5" rel="#ref-5~.ref-cit">5</a> <a id="xref-ref-6-5" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-6" rel="#ref-6~.ref-cit">6</a> <a id="xref-ref-12-11" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-12" rel="#ref-12~.ref-cit">12</a> <a id="xref-ref-20-8" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-20" rel="#ref-20~.ref-cit">20</a> <a id="xref-ref-25-8" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-25" rel="#ref-25~.ref-cit">25</a> reported marine n-3 PUFA as % energy intake/day. Dose-response analysis indicated that a 0.1g/day increment of dietary marine n-3 PUFA was associated with 5% lower risk of breast cancer (relative risk 0.95, 95% confidence interval 0.90 to 1.00, I<sup>2</sup>=52%). A 0.1% energy increment of daily dietary marine n-3 PUFA was inversely associated 5% reduction of risk (0.95, 0.90 to 1.00, I<sup>2</sup>=79%) (fig 4<a id="xref-fig-4-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#F4"><span>⇓</span></a>). There was no significant curvilinear association between marine n-3 PUFA (g/day) and risk (P=0.21 for non-linearity, fig 5<a id="xref-fig-5-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#F5"><span>⇓</span></a>). For studies with n-3 PUFA measured as % energy/day, however, we observed a significant curvilinear association (P=0.011 for non-linearity, fig 6<a id="xref-fig-6-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#F6"><span>⇓</span></a>).</p><div id="F4" class="fig pos-float  odd"><div class="fig-inline"><div class="highwire-figure colorbox"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="http://static.www.bmj.com/content/bmj/346/bmj.f3706/F4.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600" title="Fig 4 Dose-response meta-analysis for per 0.1g/day or 0.1% energy/day increment in intake of marine n-3 PUFA intake and risk of breast cancer. Overall relative risk calculated with random effects model" class="colorbox fragment-images colorbox-load init-colorbox-processed cboxElement" rel="gallery-fragment-images" data-figure-caption="&amp;lt;div class=&amp;quot;fig-caption&amp;quot; xmlns:xhtml=&amp;quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&amp;quot;&amp;gt;&amp;lt;/div&amp;gt;"><img class="fragment-image" src="http://static.www.bmj.com/content/bmj/346/bmj.f3706/F4.medium.gif" alt="Figure4" data-original-title="" title="" /></a></div></div><div xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" class="fig-caption"><p id="p-27" class="first-child"><strong>Fig 4</strong> Dose-response meta-analysis for per 0.1g/day or 0.1% energy/day increment in intake of marine n-3 PUFA intake and risk of breast cancer. Overall relative risk calculated with random effects model</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links list-inline"><li class="0 first"><a href="http://static.www.bmj.com/content/bmj/346/bmj.f3706/F4.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Figure4">Download figure</a></li>

P=0.21 for non-linearity, which indicates no curvilinear association" class="colorbox fragment-images colorbox-load init-colorbox-processed cboxElement" rel="gallery-fragment-images" data-figure-caption="&amp;lt;div class=&amp;quot;fig-caption&amp;quot; xmlns:xhtml=&amp;quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&amp;quot;&amp;gt;&amp;lt;/div&amp;gt;"><img class="fragment-image" src="http://static.www.bmj.com/content/bmj/346/bmj.f3706/F5.medium.gif" alt="Figure5" data-original-title="" title="" /></a></div></div><div xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" class="fig-caption"><p id="p-28" class="first-child"><strong>Fig 5</strong> Dose-response analysis for curvilinear association between marine n-3 PUFA intake (g/day) and risk of breast cancer. P=0.21 for non-linearity, which indicates no curvilinear association</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links list-inline"><li class="0 first"><a href="http://static.www.bmj.com/content/bmj/346/bmj.f3706/F5.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Figure5">Download figure</a></li>
<li class="1"><a href="http://static.www.bmj.com/content/bmj/346/bmj.f3706/F5.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank">Open in new tab</a></li>

</ul></div></div><div class="fig-caption" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"></div></div><div id="F6" class="fig pos-float  odd"><div class="fig-inline"><div class="highwire-figure colorbox"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="http://static.www.bmj.com/content/bmj/346/bmj.f3706/F6.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600" title="Fig 6 Dose-response analysis for curvilinear association between marine n-3 PUFA intake (% energy/day) and risk of breast cancer. Shaded area represents 95% confidence limits for fitted curve. P=0.011 for non-linearity, which indicates no curvilinear association" class="colorbox fragment-images colorbox-load init-colorbox-processed cboxElement" rel="gallery-fragment-images" data-figure-caption="&amp;lt;div class=&amp;quot;fig-caption&amp;quot; xmlns:xhtml=&amp;quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&amp;quot;&amp;gt;&amp;lt;/div&amp;gt;"><img class="fragment-image" src="http://static.www.bmj.com/content/bmj/346/bmj.f3706/F6.medium.gif" alt="Figure6" data-original-title="" title="" /></a></div></div><div xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" class="fig-caption"><p id="p-29" class="first-child"><strong>Fig 6</strong> Dose-response analysis for curvilinear association between marine n-3 PUFA intake (% energy/day) and risk of breast cancer. Shaded area represents 95% confidence limits for fitted curve. P=0.011 for non-linearity, which indicates no curvilinear association</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links list-inline"><li class="0 first"><a href="http://static.www.bmj.com/content/bmj/346/bmj.f3706/F6.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Figure6">Download figure</a></li>
<li class="1"><a href="http://static.www.bmj.com/content/bmj/346/bmj.f3706/F6.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank">Open in new tab</a></li>
<li class="2 last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/185153" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt">Download powerpoint</a></li>
</ul></div></div><div class="fig-caption" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"></div></div><p id="p-30">We then summarised the relative risks for EPA, DHA, and DPA from identified studies. Ten articles<a id="xref-ref-8-8" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-8" rel="#ref-8~.ref-cit">8</a> <a id="xref-ref-17-8" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-17" rel="#ref-17~.ref-cit">17</a> <a id="xref-ref-18-8" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-18" rel="#ref-18~.ref-cit">18</a> <a id="xref-ref-19-10" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-19" rel="#ref-19~.ref-cit">19</a> <a id="xref-ref-23-9" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-23" rel="#ref-23~.ref-cit">23</a> <a id="xref-ref-24-8" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-24" rel="#ref-24~.ref-cit">24</a> <a id="xref-ref-26-6" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-26" rel="#ref-26~.ref-cit">26</a> <a id="xref-ref-37-6" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-37" rel="#ref-37~.ref-cit">37</a> <a id="xref-ref-38-5" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-38" rel="#ref-38~.ref-cit">38</a> <a id="xref-ref-44-6" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-44" rel="#ref-44~.ref-cit">44</a> reported relative risk for both EPA and DHA, while four articles<a id="xref-ref-19-11" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-19" rel="#ref-19~.ref-cit">19</a> <a id="xref-ref-24-9" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-24" rel="#ref-24~.ref-cit">24</a> <a id="xref-ref-26-7" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-26" rel="#ref-26~.ref-cit">26</a> <a id="xref-ref-44-7" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-44" rel="#ref-44~.ref-cit">44</a> reported relative risk for DPA. There were marginally significant inverse associations for EPA (relative risk 0.93, 95% confidence interval 0.85 to 1.02) and DHA (0.88, 0.75 to 1.03) and risk. No significant association was found for DPA and risk (see supplemental table D in appendix 3).</p></div><div id="sec-11" class="subsection"><h3>ALA, total n-3 PUFA, and risk of breast cancer </h3><p id="p-31">Twelve articles, involving 14 284 breast cancer events and 405 592 participants, were included in the analysis of association between ALA exposure and risk of breast cancer. We found no significant association between ALA and risk (relative risk 0.97, 95% confidence interval 0.90 to 1.04) (see supplemental fig E in appendix 4) and no study heterogeneity (I<sup>2</sup>=0) or publication bias (P=0.37 for Egger’s test) (see supplemental fig F in appendix 4). Dose-response analysis showed no significant association with breast cancer per 0.1 g/day increment of dietary ALA intake (0.99, 0.98 to 1.01) or per 0.1% energy/day increment of dietary ALA intake (1.00, 0.99 to 1.00) (see supplemental fig G in appendix 4). No curvilinear association was observed for dietary ALA intake and risk (data not shown).</p><p id="p-32">Ten articles reported relative risk for total n-3 PUFA and risk of breast cancer, and there was no significant association (0.96, 0.86 to 1.06, I<sup>2</sup>=13%). Slight publication bias was observed (P=0.04 for Eggers test; see supplemental fig H in appendix 4); however the results remained unchanged after trim and fill analysis.</p></div><div id="sec-12" class="subsection"><h3>Subgroup analysis</h3><p id="p-33">For fish intake and ALA exposure, meta-regression and subgroup analyses did not show any substantial change in the summary relative risk (table<a id="xref-table-wrap-1-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#T1"><span>⇓</span></a>, and supplemental table E in appendix 3). Exclusion of any individual study did not change the results. Total n-3 PUFA, however, was significantly inversely associated with risk (relative risk 0.77, 95% confidence interval 0.60 to 0.99) only in studies without adjustment for BMI, and no association was observed for studies with adjustment for BMI.</p><div class="table pos-float" id="T1"><div class="table"><div class="table-caption"><p id="p-34" class="first-child">Subgroup analyses of intake of fish and marine n-3 polyunsaturated fatty acids (PUFA) and risk of breast cancer (highest versus lowest category)</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div><div class="table-inline"><div class="callout"><span>View this table:</span><ul class="callout-links"><li><a href="/highwire/markup/185154/expansion?width=1000&amp;height=500&amp;iframe=true&amp;postprocessors=highwire_figures%2Chighwire_math" class="colorbox colorbox-load table-expand-popup init-colorbox-processed cboxElement" rel="gallery-fragment-tables">View popup</a></li><li><a href="##" class="table-expand-inline highwireTablesMarkupProcessor-processed" data-table-url="/highwire/markup/185154/expansion?postprocessors=highwire_figures%2Chighwire_math">View inline</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div></div></div><p id="p-36">For total marine n-3 PUFA, the inverse association was present in both Asian countries and western countries, though it was more evident in Asian countries (relative risk 0.69, 95% confidence interval 0.56 to 0.85) and no study heterogeneity was observed (I<sup>2</sup>=0) (table<a id="xref-table-wrap-1-2" class="xref-up-link" href="#T1"><span>⇑</span></a>). Stratified by different measurement methods of n-3 PUFA, the relative risk for dietary n-3 PUFA was similar to that of tissue biomarkers, while study heterogeneity was much lower in studies measured as biomarkers (I<sup>2</sup>=8% for diet <em>v</em> 67% for biomarker). The inverse association between marine n-3 PUFA and risk was more evident in studies without adjustment for BMI (0.74, 0.64 to 0.86) compared with studies with such adjustment (0.90, 0.80 to 1.01).</p><p id="p-37">For individual marine n-3 PUFA, the significant inverse association with risk of breast cancer was observed only in studies with shorter follow-up (relative risk 0.82 (95% confidence interval 0.70 to 0.96) for EPA and 0.74 (0.62 to 0.89) for DHA; see supplemental table D in appendix 3), while it was not significant for either n-3 PUFA among studies with longer follow-up, which could be attributed to the limited number of studies with long follow-up. In addition, for both EPA and DHA exposure, their inverse associations with risk were more evident in studies that adjusted for BMI or education compared with studies without such adjustment.</p></div></div><div class="section discussion" id="sec-13"><h2>Discussion</h2><p id="p-38">In this meta-analysis dietary intake of marine n-3 polyunsaturated fatty acids (PUFA), but not alpha linolenic acid (ALA), was associated with a lower risk of breast cancer. Fish consumption was not associated with risk. Dose-response analyses indicated a 5% lower risk of breast cancer per 0.1g/day or 0.1% energy/day increment of dietary marine n-3 PUFA, but no significant trend for ALA or fish intake. To the best of our knowledge, this is the first time meta-analysis has systematically and quantitatively evaluated the association between intake of fish and n-3 PUFA and risk of breast cancer.</p><div id="sec-14" class="subsection"><h3>Results in relation to other studies</h3><p id="p-39">Ecological studies<a id="xref-ref-46-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-46" rel="#ref-46~.ref-cit">46</a> <a id="xref-ref-47-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-47" rel="#ref-47~.ref-cit">47</a> and prospective cohort studies<a id="xref-ref-12-12" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-12" rel="#ref-12~.ref-cit">12</a> <a id="xref-ref-28-4" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-28" rel="#ref-28~.ref-cit">28</a> <a id="xref-ref-29-5" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-29" rel="#ref-29~.ref-cit">29</a> have suggested an inverse association between fish consumption and risk of breast cancer and mortality. Marine n-3 PUFA (EPA, DHA, and DPA) are abundant in fish fat, and a few large prospective cohort studies<a id="xref-ref-5-10" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-5" rel="#ref-5~.ref-cit">5</a> <a id="xref-ref-12-13" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-12" rel="#ref-12~.ref-cit">12</a> <a id="xref-ref-27-7" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-27" rel="#ref-27~.ref-cit">27</a> and case-control studies<a id="xref-ref-13-2" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-13" rel="#ref-13~.ref-cit">13</a> <a id="xref-ref-14-2" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-14" rel="#ref-14~.ref-cit">14</a> <a id="xref-ref-15-2" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-15" rel="#ref-15~.ref-cit">15</a> <a id="xref-ref-16-2" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-16" rel="#ref-16~.ref-cit">16</a> have reported an inverse association with risk of breast cancer. These findings all agreed with one meta-analysis<a id="xref-ref-4-2" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-4" rel="#ref-4~.ref-cit">4</a> based on studies that assessed the biomarkers of intakes of dietary fatty acids, which suggested a potential protective effect of marine n-3 PUFA on breast cancer. Another systematic review suggested that there was no protective association between n-3 PUFA and breast cancer.<a id="xref-ref-7-2" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-7" rel="#ref-7~.ref-cit">7</a> Further evidence has been published since then, and quite a few of the newly published prospective studies indicated potential protective effects of n-3 PUFA on breast cancer.<a id="xref-ref-27-8" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-27" rel="#ref-27~.ref-cit">27</a> <a id="xref-ref-38-6" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-38" rel="#ref-38~.ref-cit">38</a> <a id="xref-ref-43-6" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-43" rel="#ref-43~.ref-cit">43</a> Our meta-analysis based on this evidence, together with previous publications, supports a protective role of marine n-3 PUFA on the incidence of breast cancer.</p><p id="p-40">Subgroup analysis indicated that the protective effect of marine n-3 PUFA was more evident in Asian countries than in the US or European countries. Fish intake also tended to be associated with a lower risk of breast cancer in Asian populations, rather than in western populations. This could be because typical fish intake is much higher in Asian populations than in western populations.<a id="xref-ref-48-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-48" rel="#ref-48~.ref-cit">48</a> Therefore fish intake in these western populations might be too low to detect an expected protective effect. Furthermore, in North America and some European countries, a large proportion of intake of marine n-3 PUFA probably comes from fish oil supplementation in the form of capsules, thereby contributing to the different effects on risk. In addition, the protective effect of fish intake might be attenuated or even reversed by other constituents in fish, such as organometallics and pesticides. Taken together, these factors could explain our finding of an overall null association between fish intake and risk of breast cancer, which was in line with a previous large pooled analysis.<a id="xref-ref-49-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-49" rel="#ref-49~.ref-cit">49</a></p><p id="p-41">Tissue n-3 PUFA concentrations, compared with dietary assessment, might provide a more accurate estimation of intake. Subgroup analysis for marine n-3 PUFA and risk, however, indicated that the summary risk estimate for studies with dietary information was similar to that of tissue biomarkers, which further confirmed the robust results of the present meta-analysis. Further concerns regarding n-3 PUFA and risk are menopausal status and hormone receptor status (oestrogen receptor and progesterone receptor). Previous cohort studies indicated that the protective effect of marine n-3 PUFA against breast cancer was more evident in postmenopausal women than in premenopausal women.<a id="xref-ref-12-14" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-12" rel="#ref-12~.ref-cit">12</a> <a id="xref-ref-19-12" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-19" rel="#ref-19~.ref-cit">19</a> Our meta-analysis confirmed that n-3 PUFA intake was significantly inversely associated with breast cancer in postmenopausal but not in premenopausal women; this could mean that any benefit of marine n-3 PUFA is usually after long term exposure, which could be observed best at the postmenopausal period because breast cancer is a disease with a long latency between exposure and development. Another explanation could be related to the different effects of body fat on premenopausal and postmenopausal risk of breast cancer. One recent meta-analysis suggested that high BMI tends to be protective against premenopausal breast cancer but is a risk factor for postmenopausal breast cancer,<a id="xref-ref-50-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-50" rel="#ref-50~.ref-cit">50</a> and an interaction between BMI and menopausal status on breast cancer has been proposed. Therefore, marine n-3 PUFA could influence risk through BMI, which was supported by our subgroup analysis. The inverse association between marine n-3 PUFA and risk was greatly attenuated in studies that adjusted for BMI compared with studies without such adjustment. Most of the studies that investigated marine n-3 PUFA intake and postmenopausal breast cancer, however, did adjust for BMI, and an overall significant inverse association between marine n-3 PUFA and risk still existed in these postmenopausal studies. This suggests that the effect of marine n-3 PUFA on risk was partly independent of BMI, and a more precise mechanism for this discrepancy remains to be investigated. In addition, only a few studies examined the influence of oestrogen receptor and progesterone receptor status on the association between fish intake and risk of breast cancer. Stripp and colleagues reported an adverse effect of fish consumption only for oestrogen receptor positive breast cancer in a cohort of postmenopausal women.<a id="xref-ref-33-5" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-33" rel="#ref-33~.ref-cit">33</a> Two other studies based on large prospective cohorts found no evidence for the influence of hormone receptor status on n-3 PUFA and risk.<a id="xref-ref-6-6" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-6" rel="#ref-6~.ref-cit">6</a> <a id="xref-ref-8-9" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-8" rel="#ref-8~.ref-cit">8</a> More prospective studies are warranted to investigate the impact of hormone receptor status.</p><p id="p-42">In contrast with marine n-3 PUFA, the effect of ALA, a plant based n-3 PUFA, on breast tumour growth is less clear, and we found no significant association. The explanation for the inconsistency among studies regarding dietary ALA and risk of breast cancer could be the different dietary sources. Thiebaut and colleagues showed that dietary ALA from fruit and vegetables and vegetable oils was inversely associated with risk but observed a positive association for ALA from nut mixes and processed meat.<a id="xref-ref-25-9" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-25" rel="#ref-25~.ref-cit">25</a> In addition, the biological effect of ALA on breast cancer per se might be not as strong as marine n-3 PUFA, as suggested by the summarised relative risk of biomarker data with no significant association in our study. Taken together, we found no significant protective association with ALA, and the inconsistent associations observed among previous studies might reflect different dietary/food patterns involving other nutrients related to risk of breast cancer.</p><p id="p-43">The anticarcinogenic effects of marine n-3 PUFA are biologically plausible. Possible mechanisms include inhibition of eicosanoid derived from arachidonic acid, regulation of transcription factor activity, gene expression and activities of molecules involved in the signal transduction of cell growth, differentiation apoptosis, angiogenesis, and metastasis.<a id="xref-ref-51-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-51" rel="#ref-51~.ref-cit">51</a> In addition, marine n-3 PUFA could decrease the production of oestrogen, thus reducing oestrogen stimulated cell growth.<a id="xref-ref-51-2" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-51" rel="#ref-51~.ref-cit">51</a> Specifically, studies using cell lines and on rodent models have shown the protective effects of marine n-3 PUFA against breast tumour growth.<a id="xref-ref-9-2" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-9" rel="#ref-9~.ref-cit">9</a> <a id="xref-ref-10-2" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-10" rel="#ref-10~.ref-cit">10</a> <a id="xref-ref-11-2" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-11" rel="#ref-11~.ref-cit">11</a> <a id="xref-ref-52-1" class="xref-bibr article-ref-popup-processed" href="#ref-52" rel="#ref-52~.ref-cit">52</a></p></div><div id="sec-15" class="subsection"><h3>Strengths and limitations</h3><p id="p-44">The present meta-analysis has several strengths. Firstly, the large sample size allowed us to quantitatively assess the association of fish and n-3 PUFA intake and risk of breast cancer, thus making it more powerful than any individual study. Secondly, the prospective nature of the included studies avoided the influence of recall and selection bias. Thirdly, we systematically reviewed and assessed the summarised association between breast cancer with different types of individual n-3 PUFA, including EPA, DHA, DPA, and ALA. These data gave a most comprehensive view of the association between n-3 fatty acids and risk based on the current evidence.</p><p id="p-45">The meta-analysis does, however, also have several limitations. Firstly, different methods of assessment (diet and tissue biomarker) were used in the included studies, and the units were heterogeneous across different studies. Nevertheless, we used relative risks for the highest versus lowest category of n-3 or fish intake, which could, to some extent, reduce the bias caused by different units or exposure assessment methods. Furthermore, dose-response analysis supports our results. Secondly, available data on the individual n-3 PUFA, especially DPA, is rather limited. Therefore, future prospective studies are needed for the detailed analysis of association between individual n-3 PUFA and risk of breast cancer. Thirdly, the observational nature of the included studies makes it subject to the influence of residual confounders. In addition, possible language bias could occur because we excluded articles not in English. Our eligible articles, however, covered a wide range of non-English countries, such as countries across Europe and Asia, and the number of large cohorts in other non-English countries is limited.</p></div><div id="sec-16" class="subsection"><h3>Conclusions</h3><p id="p-46">Our findings have important public health implications. The prevention of breast cancer continues to be an important public health issue for researchers, especially with regard to the investigation of relations between breast cancer, diet, and lifestyle. Evidence from either experimental or observational studies suggests a protective effect of marine n-3 PUFA on breast cancer, though no conclusive results have been achieved. Systematic review and meta-analysis are the most powerful tools to assess these kinds of inconsistent associations. Therefore, our present study provides solid and robust evidence that marine n-3 PUFA are inversely associated with risk of breast cancer. The protective effect of fish or individual n-3 PUFA warrants further investigation of prospective studies.</p><div class="style3" id="boxed-text-1"><div id="sec-17" class="subsection"><h4>What is already known on this topic</h4><ul class="list-simple " id="list-1"><li id="list-item-1"><p id="p-47">Breast cancer is one of the most common cancers and the leading cause of cancer death among women in the world</p></li><li id="list-item-2"><p id="p-48">Epidemiological studies suggest that fish and n-3 polyunsaturated fatty acids intake have a protective role in breast cancer, though results from prospective studies are inconsistent</p></li></ul></div><div id="sec-18" class="subsection"><h4>What this study adds</h4><ul class="list-simple " id="list-2"><li id="list-item-3"><p id="p-49">High intake of marine n-3 polyunsaturated fatty acids is associated with 14% reduction in risk of breast cancer</p></li><li id="list-item-4"><p id="p-50">Each 0.1 g/day or 0.1% energy/day increment of intake was associated with a 5% reduction in risk</p></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="section notes" id="notes-2"><h2>Notes</h2><p id="p-57"><strong>Cite this as:</strong> <em>BMJ</em> 2013;346:f3706</p></div><div class="section fn-group" id="fn-group-1"><h2>Footnotes</h2><ul><li class="fn" id="fn-1"><p id="p-51">We thank Laurence D Parnell at Jean Mayer USDA Human Nutrition Research Center on Aging at Tufts University for helping to edit the manuscript.</p></li><li class="fn-participating-researchers" id="fn-2"><p id="p-52">Contributors: J-SZ and DL conceived the study. <div id="altmetric">
	<script src="https://d1bxh8uas1mnw7.cloudfront.net/assets/embed.js"></script><script>
   jQuery(function($) {
     function go() {
       var original_callback = window._altmetric.embed_callback,
         doi = $("meta[name=citation_doi]").attr("content");

       window._altmetric.embed_callback = function(e) {
         $("#altmetric-header").remove();
         $("#altmetric")
           .prepend("&lt;div id='altmetric-header'&gt;" +
             "&lt;img src='http://journals.bmj.com/site/images/logo-altmetric.png' /&gt;" +
             "&lt;p&gt;Who is talking about this article?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/div&gt;");

         window._altmetric.embed_callback = original_callback;

         return original_callback(e);
       };

       $("#altmetric")
         .html("&lt;div class='altmetric-embed' data-badge-type='medium-donut' data-badge-details='right' data-hide-no-mentions='true'&gt;&lt;/div&gt;");

       _altmetric_embed_init();
     }

     function check() {
       if (typeof _altmetric === "undefined") {
         window.setTimeout(check, 1000);
       }
       else {
         go();
       }
     }

     check();
   });
 </script></div>
